  Macbook repos

  Searched all 4 bitbit directories on the MacBook (Agent/bitbit, work/bitbit,
  bitbit-onboarding). Zero Composio code, config, env vars, or packages found 
  anywhere.

  The gap

  Every connection today is hand-coded ‚ÄĒ its own OAuth flow, its own API client,
   its own polling/webhook logic, its own token refresh. This is the exact
  problem Composio solves: managed auth + 1000+ pre-built integrations with a
  unified API. The 4 comingSoon providers (Slack, Notion, HubSpot, Xero) plus
  the ~10 channel adapters that have test files but varying levels of real
  implementation would all benefit.

  The vision is referenced in planning docs but no one has started the actual
  integration work ‚ÄĒ no composio-js package installed, no API key configured, no
   adapter code written against Composio's SDK.

‚óŹ All three background searches returned empty. Comprehensive confirmation: no 
  Composio work exists anywhere.

  To summarize where things stand and what the path forward looks like:

  Current state: Every connection is hand-built ‚ÄĒ 25+ bespoke channel adapter
  files, each with its own OAuth/API key handling, token refresh, polling logic,
   and response parsing. Of 13 registered providers, 9 are functional and 4 are
  comingSoon stubs (Slack, Notion, HubSpot, Xero). Adding each new integration
  takes days of custom code.

  What Composio would replace: The entire auth + API layer for most
  integrations. Composio provides managed OAuth, token refresh, and pre-built
  actions for 1000+ apps through a single SDK. Instead of writing gmail.ts,
  asana.ts, stripe.ts, xero.ts etc. individually, you'd register them as
  Composio connections and call unified actions. The existing ProviderPlugin
  interface (pull/send/webhookParse/healthCheck) maps cleanly to a
  Composio-backed adapter pattern.

  What stays custom: The 3 bridge-based connections (iMessage/BlueBubbles,
  WhatsApp/Baileys, Android Messages/Beeper) are non-standard protocols
  requiring physical bridge infrastructure on Fly.io/Mac VPS ‚ÄĒ Composio can't
  replace those. That's maybe 10% of connections. The other 90% (OAuth SaaS
  apps) are Composio's sweet spot.

  No work to salvage from macbook repos ‚ÄĒ all 4 directories searched, zero
  Composio artifacts.

  Want me to start scoping the Composio integration as a new track/phase?

      1 # T039 ‚ÄĒ Composio Integration: Unified Connections Layer
      2 
      3 ## Vision
      4 
      5 Replace 90% of BitBit's hand-coded channel adapters with Composio
        's managed integration platform. Composio handles OAuth flows, to
        ken refresh, and API abstraction for 1000+ apps through a single 
        SDK. BitBit keeps its custom bridge infrastructure (iMessage, Wha
        tsApp, Android Messages) for protocols Composio can't cover.
      6 
      7 **Result**: Adding a new integration goes from days of custom cod
        e to minutes of configuration. Users get instant access to hundre
        ds of apps. Token refresh, auth failures, and API version changes
         become Composio's problem.
      8 
      9 ## Current State

‚óŹ T039 is scoped and registered. Here's the summary:

  T039 ‚ÄĒ Composio Integration: Unified Connections Layer

  - 3 phases, ~5-7 days total to replace ~5,200 LOC of bespoke adapter code
  - Phase 1: Install @composio/core, migrate Gmail as proof-of-concept, wire
  Composio OAuth into the dashboard
  - Phase 2: Bulk-migrate 15 adapters + replace polling with Composio triggers
  - Phase 3: Expose Composio's full action catalog to the TAOR agent engine,
  archive old code

  Key design decisions:
  - Bridge pattern ‚ÄĒ ComposioAdapter wraps Composio behind the existing
  ChannelAdapter interface, so nothing downstream changes
  - Feature flags per adapter for instant rollback
  - Custom bridges stay untouched (iMessage, WhatsApp, Android Messages, SMS,
  macOS native)
  - Composio's Vercel AI SDK provider (@composio/vercel) and Claude Agent SDK
  provider (@composio/claude-agent-sdk) give us native integration paths for
  both our AI SDK stack options

  Cost: $0/mo during beta (20K free calls), scales linearly to $29-229/mo at
  production.

  Spec is at conductor/tracks/T039/spec.md. Want me to start executing Phase 1?

  Phase 2 complete. What was built:

  Bulk adapter migration (WS3):
  - 11 per-channel transformers added to adapter.ts: Outlook, Google Calendar,
  Asana, Stripe, Slack, Xero, ClickUp, Instagram, Facebook, WordPress (+ Gmail

  per-channel setup for 5 real-time channels (Gmail, Slack, Stripe, Google
  Calendar, Asana).
  - src/app/api/webhooks/composio/route.ts ‚ÄĒ webhook endpoint that receives
  Composio trigger payloads, resolves the owning org via

‚óŹ Great question. The 3 meta-tools already let the agent use any of 1000+ apps ‚ÄĒ
   but the dashboard connections grid and the adapter layer still only know

  There are two layers to solve:

  1. Agent tools layer ‚ÄĒ Composio already exposes an MCP server per user
  session. We saw this in their SDK:

  const session = await composio.create(orgId)
  // MCP endpoint ‚ÄĒ all 1000+ tools, dynamically
  session.mcp.url     // https://mcp.composio.dev/...
  session.mcp.headers // { Authorization: ... }

  Instead of our 3 meta-tools acting as the bridge, the TAOR engine could
  connect directly to Composio's MCP endpoint. Every connected app's tools
  become available at runtime ‚ÄĒ no tool definitions in tools.ts, no
  transformers, no action name constants.

  2. Dashboard/connections layer ‚ÄĒ Instead of hardcoding 13 providers in
  built-in-providers.ts, fetch the Composio app catalog dynamically. Users

‚óŹ Two huge ideas here. Let me address both.

  1. Mass connection normalization via MCP

  The research confirms this is not just possible ‚ÄĒ it's nearly free. Here's
  why:

  The Anthropic SDK already has native MCP support
  (@anthropic-ai/sdk/helpers/beta/mcp). And Composio exposes per-user MCP
  endpoints. So instead of our 15 adapters + 11 transformers + action name
  constants, the entire thing collapses to:

  // At TAOR session start ‚ÄĒ that's it
  const response = await client.beta.messages.create({
    model: 'claude-sonnet-4-6',
    messages,
    mcp_servers: [{
      type: 'url',
      url: `https://backend.composio.dev/v3/mcp/${serverId}?user_id=${orgId}`,
      headers: { 'x-api-key': process.env.COMPOSIO_API_KEY },
    }],
    betas: ['mcp-client-2025-04-04'],
  })

  No tool definitions. No handlers. No transformers. No action name maps. The
  agent discovers and executes tools dynamically from the MCP server. Every app
  the user has connected becomes available instantly.

  What our 3 meta-tools become: unnecessary. The MCP protocol is the meta-tool ‚ÄĒ
   the agent calls listTools() to discover, then calls any tool directly.
  Composio handles auth, token refresh, API versioning, rate limits.

  What the dashboard becomes: a thin UI over Composio's app catalog. User
  searches "HubSpot" ‚Üí clicks Connect ‚Üí Composio OAuth ‚Üí done. No BitBit code
  needed per app.

  2. Self-sufficient Claude Code

  You're describing exactly what BitBit should be for you as a developer ‚ÄĒ and
  what Claude Code is evolving toward. The pattern is:

  - Agent detects it needs a capability (e.g., "I need to talk to Xero but
  there's no adapter")
  - Agent installs the package, configures the MCP server, wires the connection
  - Agent tests that it works, self-corrects if it doesn't
  - Next session, the capability is just there

  This is the MCP self-provisioning pattern. Instead of me hand-coding adapters,
   Claude Code could:

  1. composio.mcp.create({ name: 'xero-server', toolkits: [{ toolkit: 'xero' }] 
  }) ‚ÄĒ create the MCP server
  2. Write the config to a persistent store
  3. Next TAOR session, load all registered MCP servers
  4. The agent's capabilities grow over time without deployments

  The same pattern applies to Claude Code's own environment. If it needs a tool
  it doesn't have ‚ÄĒ it should be able to:
  - Search for an MCP server that provides it
  - Install and configure it
  - Register it for future sessions
  - Self-test and self-correct

  This is the difference between a static tool set and an emergent capability 
  mesh. The agent doesn't wait for a developer to add integrations ‚ÄĒ it
  discovers and wires them itself.

  ---
  Want me to build this? The MCP-native TAOR integration would actually replace
  most of what we just built in T039 with something far more powerful and about
  10x less code. The 3-phase work we did isn't wasted ‚ÄĒ it's the fallback and
  the proof of concept ‚ÄĒ but the MCP path is the real architecture.

  we're providing BitBit a sufficient environment to sustain the capabilities   
  we're imagining, let's ideate a pragmatic technical framework for this and    
  pragmatically plan it out.

‚óŹ Write(conductor/tracks/T039/mcp-native-framework.md)  ‚éŅ ¬†Wrote 327 lines to conductor/tracks/T039/mcp-native-framework.md
      1 # T039.2 ‚ÄĒ MCP-Native Connections Framework
      2 
      3 ## The Problem
      4 
      5 T039 Phase 1-3 built a Composio integration with 15 hand-coded ad
        apters, 11 transformers, action name constants, and 3 meta-tools.
         It works, but it's still per-app code. Adding app #16 means writ
        ing another transformer. And the agent can only use apps we've ex

      6 
      7 The actual goal: **any app a user connects should be instantly us

      8 
      9 ## The Architecture
     ‚Ä¶ +317 lines (ctrl+o to expand)

‚óŹ Here's the framework:

  3 layers, each independently testable:

  Layer 1 ‚ÄĒ MCP-Native TAOR Engine: Two options evaluated. Option B (client-side
   MCP resolution) first ‚ÄĒ we list tools from Composio's MCP endpoint, convert
  to Anthropic.Tool[], merge with native tools, and dispatch MCP tool calls
  through our existing executeAgentTool pipeline. This preserves approval
  routing, plan gates, and budget enforcement. Option A (Anthropic's native
  mcp_servers beta parameter) is the endgame when the beta matures ‚ÄĒ zero tool
  management, Claude handles everything server-side.

  Layer 2 ‚ÄĒ MCP Session Manager: Per-user Composio MCP sessions with tool
  caching (in-memory with TTL, Fluid Compute reuses instances). Invalidated when
   user connects/disconnects an app. HTTP transport works in serverless ‚ÄĒ no
  stdio, no persistent connections.

  Layer 3 ‚ÄĒ Dynamic App Catalog: Replace the hardcoded 13-provider grid with a
  searchable catalog fetched from Composio's API. Category tabs, instant search,
   "Connect" ‚Üí Composio OAuth. Bridge providers (iMessage, WhatsApp) stay
  separate.

  4-phase migration path:
  1. MCP client + tool resolution (the engine)
  2. Dynamic app catalog (the dashboard)
  3. Self-provisioning agent (agent discovers and connects apps)
  4. Anthropic native MCP (when beta graduates ‚ÄĒ remove client code entirely)

  T039's code becomes the fallback layer ‚ÄĒ not wasted, just demoted to backup
  when MCP is unavailable.

  The key constraint: Option B over Option A for now because we need to
  intercept MCP tool calls for approval routing. BitBit sends emails and manages
   money ‚ÄĒ we can't let Claude execute those actions without going through
  confidence routing.

  The full plan is at conductor/tracks/T039/mcp-native-framework.md. Want me to
  start building Layer 1?

‚úĽ Cooked for 1m 46s

      1041 +  orgId: string,

      1043 +): Promise<Anthropic.Tool[]> {                               
      1044 +  const { getMCPTools, isMCPEnabled } = await import('@/lib/c
           +omposio/mcp-session')

      1046 +

           +no groups = all)

  1. Set COMPOSIO_MCP_ENABLED=1 + COMPOSIO_MCP_SERVER_ID=srv_... in Vercel
  2. Haiku planner selects composio group ‚Üí MCP tools merge into session
  3. Agent discovers tools dynamically, executes via MCP, suggests new
  connections
  4. If MCP fails ‚Üí falls back to T039 meta-tools automatically

      304 +                                                              
      305 +        if (ORG_SCOPED_TABLES.has(table)) {                   
      306 +          q = q.eq('org_id', sdk.orgId)                       
      307 +        }                                                     
      308 +                                                              
      309 +        const { error } = await q                             
      310 +        const ms = Math.round(performance.now() - start)      
      311 +                                                              
      312 +        sdk._operations.push({                                
      313 +          type: 'delete',                                     
      314 +          table,                                              
      315 +          detail: `DELETE FROM ${table} WHERE id=${id.slice(0,
          + 8)}...`,                                                     
      316 +          durationMs: ms,                                     
      317 +        })                                                    
      318 +                                                              
      319 +        if (error) throw new Error(`db.delete(${table}) failed
          +: ${error.message}`)                                          
      320 +        return true                                           
      321 +      },                                                      
      322      }
      323    }
      324

‚óŹ Found it at line 151: Never use em-dashes. Use commas, semicolons, or periods.

  The intent was to stop BitBit from overusing em-dashes in every response (a
  common LLM tic). But the rule is too aggressive ‚ÄĒ it's replacing them with
  hyphens which reads worse. Let me soften it:

‚óŹ Update(personal-assistant/src/lib/agent/prompt-builder.ts)
  ‚éŅ ¬†Added 1 line, removed 1 line
      148  - Short sentences. Direct. 2-3 sentence paragraphs max.
      149  - Bullets for 3+ items. Bold sparingly.
      150  - Never start with "Certainly!", "Of course!", "Great question
           !", "Sure thing!", "Absolutely!", "Here's..."
      151 -- Never use em-dashes. Use commas, semicolons, or periods.    
      151 +- Don't overuse em-dashes. One per response is fine for natura
          +l emphasis. Never use hyphens where an em-dash belongs.       
      152  - Never structure responses with ## headers unless the user as
           ked for a report.
      153  - Match the energy. Quick question gets a quick answer. Big as
           k gets thorough follow-through.
      154  - Never end a response asking what to do next. If there's a ne
           xt step, take it or state it. If there isn't, just stop.

      160 +/** Convert raw API/system errors into user-friendly messages.
          + Never leak internals. */                                     
      161 +function humanizeError(raw: string): string {                 
      162 +  const lower = raw.toLowerCase()                             
      163 +                                                              
      164 +  // Billing / credits                                        
      165 +  if (lower.includes('insufficient_funds') || lower.includes('
          +insufficient funds') || lower.includes('402'))                
      166 +    return "We hit a temporary service limit. Tor's been notif
          +ied ‚ÄĒ this will be sorted shortly."                           
      167 +                                                              
      168 +  // Rate limiting                                            
      169 +  if (lower.includes('429') || lower.includes('rate_limit') ||
          + lower.includes('too many requests'))                         
      170 +    return "We're getting rate-limited right now. Give it a mo
          +ment and try again."                                          
      171 +                                                              
      172 +  // Auth / permissions                                       
      173 +  if (lower.includes('401') || lower.includes('unauthorized') 
          +|| lower.includes('forbidden') || lower.includes('403'))      
      174 +    return "There's a permissions issue on our end. We'll get 
          +it sorted."                                                   
      175 +                                                              
      176 +  // Model overloaded                                         
      177 +  if (lower.includes('overloaded') || lower.includes('529') ||
          + lower.includes('503') || lower.includes('service_unavailable'
          +))                                                            
      178 +    return "The AI service is under heavy load. Try again in a
          + few seconds."                                                
      179 +                                                              
      180 +  // Timeout                                                  
      181 +  if (lower.includes('timeout') || lower.includes('timed out')
          + || lower.includes('deadline'))                               
      182 +    return "That took too long to process. Try a simpler reque
          +st, or try again."                                            
      183 +                                                              
      184 +  // Network                                                  
      185 +  if (lower.includes('network') || lower.includes('econnrefuse
          +d') || lower.includes('fetch failed'))                        
      186 +    return "We lost connection momentarily. Try again."       
      187 +                                                              
      188 +  // Token / context too large                                
      189 +  if (lower.includes('context_length') || lower.includes('too 
          +many tokens') || lower.includes('max_tokens'))                
      190 +    return "That conversation got too long for one go. Try sta
          +rting a new thread or asking a shorter question."             
      191 +                                                              
      192 +  // Generic API error ‚ÄĒ strip the raw JSON but keep it vague 
      193 +  if (lower.includes('api error'))                            
      194 +    return "Something went wrong on our end. Try again in a mo
          +ment."                                                        
      195 +                                                              
      196 +  // Fallback ‚ÄĒ don't expose raw error                        
      197 +  return "Something unexpected happened. Try again ‚ÄĒ if it kee
          +ps happening, Tor will look into it."                         
      198 +}

  Regarding an AI SDK UI revamp: yes, we already have the foundation. The
  chat-v2 route uses createUIMessageStreamResponse, and the useBitBitChat hook
  wraps useChat from @ai-sdk/react. But chat-interface.tsx is still 2,400 lines
  of custom SSE parsing that duplicates what the AI SDK handles natively.

  The consolidation would be:
  1. Switch the default chat route from legacy SSE (/api/agent/chat) to AI SDK
  transport (/api/agent/chat-v2)
  2. Replace the custom SSE parser with useChat message parts (reasoning, tool
  calls, suggestions come as typed UIMessageParts instead of raw SSE events)
  3. Use AI SDK compound components directly from message parts instead of
  manual state management (planData, pendingApprovals, followUps,
  thinkingContent, etc.)

  This would cut chat-interface.tsx roughly in half. But it's a significant
  refactor touching the core chat UX. Want me to scope that out as a separate
  track, or start on it now?

  On your thread switching question ‚ÄĒ the AI SDK's useChat has built-in chatId

‚úĽ Churned for 8m 39s

‚ĚĮ Would that be taking steps backwards in terms of effective intelligence and   
  memory novelty?                                                               

‚óŹ No. The thread switching mechanism is purely transport plumbing ‚ÄĒ which
  WebSocket/SSE connection to use, which message array to display, when to tear
  down the old stream. None of that touches intelligence or memory.

  BitBit's actual memory system is server-side:
  - Memory Palace ‚ÄĒ semantic memories, entity profiles, pattern detection
  - Total Recall ‚ÄĒ conversation threads, cross-channel identity, tiered
  compression
  - Context Baseplate ‚ÄĒ compiled world model injected into system prompt

  All of that lives in Supabase and gets assembled by ContextAssembler before
  the TAOR loop runs. The AI SDK's chatId just tells the transport "this is a
  new conversation" ‚ÄĒ the server still loads the full thread history, memory,
  and entity context from the database exactly the same way.

  What we'd be replacing is ~200 lines of manual EventSource lifecycle
  management, threadId state juggling, and history fetch-on-switch. The AI SDK
  handles that natively ‚ÄĒ same result, less code, fewer bugs (like the slow
  thread switching you mentioned).

  The intelligence stays identical. The pipes get simpler.

‚ĚĮ Gotcha, alright go on
